<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="a9f7c06e59754ccbabbb1e0615f312af"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="a9f7c06e59754ccbabbb1e0615f312af">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'a19f30e5-8f53-477b-b19d-f077ca946653'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d45934832" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>High-power-density rechargeable batteries are highly indispensable for power-intensive application scenarios, such as vehicle start-stop system and grid-level frequency regulation. However, realizing high-power-density alkali-ion batteries is challenging especially at low temperatures, as undesirable alkali metal plating tends to occur more easily on the anode surface due to the increased electrochemical impedance and polarization. Herein, a low-temperature, high-power-density rechargeable Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> ||hard carbon (HC) sodium-ion full battery without Na plating is realized by electrolyte regulation. The designed high-solvation-entropy electrolyte enables a high ionic conductivity of 13.87&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> at −40&nbsp;°C. More importantly, the enhanced contact-ion pairs solvation structure induces an anion-derived, thin, NaF-rich solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on HC anodes with low interfacial resistance to ensure facile Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion through the SEI. Consequently, the assembled sodium-ion full battery demonstrates a record-high power density of 1132.31&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (with an energy density of 61.94&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) at −40&nbsp;°C and 13372.56&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (132.83&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) at 25&nbsp;°C (based on total mass of the active materials on anode and cathode). This work will expedite the development of sodium-ion batteries for power-intensive applications in extreme environments.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315498-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315498-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>High-power-density rechargeable batteries are essential for power-intensive application scenarios such as electric construction machineries, electric drones, vehicle start-stop systems, grid-level frequency regulation, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0001_R_d45934824e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0004_R_d45934824e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nature">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, conventional batteries often fail to offer sufficient power input at subzero temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0005_R_d45934824e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have recently emerged as a potential candidate for cryogenic energy storage due to their superior kinetic properties,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0006_R_d45934824e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - eScience">6</a>-<a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0008_R_d45934824e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Struct.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> derived from the weaker solvent coordination capability and smaller Stokes radius of Na<sup>+</sup> in comparison to Li<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0009_R_d45934824e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0010_R_d45934824e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the power densities of the reported low-temperature SIBs are far from ideal, all fail to reach 1000&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> below −20&nbsp;°C (based on total mass of the active materials on anode and cathode).</p>
         
         <p>Realizing high-power-density SIBs at low temperatures remains highly challenging due to the following three aspects<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0011_R_d45934824e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0013_R_d45934824e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Acc. Chem. Res.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>: a) ionic conductivity of traditional electrolytes drastically declines at sub-freezing condition due to increased viscosity or even solidification<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0014_R_d45934824e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Energy">14</a>-<a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0016_R_d45934824e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>; b) interfacial resistance increases due to more difficult desolvation behavior and more sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer at low temperatures<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0017_R_d45934824e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Energy">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0018_R_d45934824e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Nano">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>; c) larger polarization is triggered by (a) and (b), which aggravates the Na plating on anodes, leading to inferior cycling stability and even safety risks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0019_R_d45934824e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0020_R_d45934824e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>As the most promising anode material for commercialization in SIBs, hard carbon (HC) possesses a low-potential plateau (≈0.1&nbsp;V) with a high capacity, which is favorable for increasing the energy density of full batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0021_R_d45934824e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a>-<a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0024_R_d45934824e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, at low temperatures and high rates, the operating potential of HC can easily drop below 0&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na due to the increased electrochemical impedance and polarization, inducing undesirable Na plating on HC anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0025_R_d45934824e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Environ. Sci.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0026_R_d45934824e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it remains arduous to realize high-power-density HC-based SIBs at low temperatures.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a high-power-density rechargeable HC-based sodium-ion full battery is successfully realized at −40&nbsp;°C through rational electrolyte chemistry (<b>Scheme</b> <a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0006">1</a>). Benefited from the high entropy and small solvation clusters of the designed electrolyte, high ionic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (t<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) can be achieved at low temperatures. Significantly, the enhanced contact-ion pairs in the designed electrolyte induce the formation of a thin, NaF-rich SEI layer on HC anode with small interfacial resistance. When paired with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP), the full battery demonstrates a record-high power density of 1132.31&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (with an energy density of 61.94&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) at −40&nbsp;°C without Na plating. This work sheds light on future development of low-temperature, high-power-density batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315498-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/884978c8-0658-4422-8148-68226723b7af/adfm202315498-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/884978c8-0658-4422-8148-68226723b7af/adfm202315498-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9082c771-d224-4702-aae1-93e35ac18a22/adfm202315498-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/884978c8-0658-4422-8148-68226723b7af/adfm202315498-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315498-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315498" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of SIBs with conventional electrolytes (left) and the designed electrolyte (right) operating at low temperatures. In conventional electrolytes, Na plating is prone to form on HC anodes at high rate and low temperature, while that can be greatly suppressed in the designed electrolyte. The colored dots in the dashed blue circle (top middle) represent individual solvent molecules or ions.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315498-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315498-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Electrochemical performance of rechargeable NVP||HC full batteries with various electrolytes including a rationally designed electrolyte (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>)-diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (G2)/1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME)) and four typical electrolytes (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-DME, 0.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-ethylene carbonate (EC)/diethyl carbonate (DEC)), have been thoroughly investigated. NVP is adopted as the cathode material due to its stable 3D open-frame structure and high-rate performance, which are beneficial for low-temperature applications (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0027_R_d45934824e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Rare Met.">27</a>-<a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0029_R_d45934824e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At −40&nbsp;°C, the NVP||HC full battery with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte exhibits high rate performance up to 5&nbsp;C (1&nbsp;C = 117&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, based on mass of active materials on cathode), achieving reversible discharge capacities of 89.20&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05&nbsp;C and 32.30&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C without Na plating on the HC anodes (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0001">1a,b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information). By comparison, the full batteries with 0.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2 and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2 electrolytes present rapid capacity fading with obvious Na plating on HC surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b,c, S3</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), and those with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-DME and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-EC/DEC electrolytes even fail in the first cycle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2d,e</a>, Supporting Information). Impressively, the full batteries with the designed electrolyte deliver record-high power densities of 1132.31&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (with an energy density of 61.94&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>), 1679.29&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (64.05&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) and 5940.90&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (67.82&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) at −40, −30, and −20&nbsp;°C, respectively, while maintaining a considerable power density of 13372.56&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (132.83&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) at 25&nbsp;°C<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0016_R_d45934824e721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0028_R_d45934824e721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a>-<a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0041_R_d45934824e721" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (all based on total mass of the active materials on anode and cathode unless otherwise specified) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0001">1d</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, a remarkable cyclability with 80.60% capacity retention after 1400 cycles at −40&nbsp;°C is presented (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0001">1e,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), which is among the top of all reported state-of-the-art Na batteries at −40&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0030_R_d45934824e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0037_R_d45934824e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0038_R_d45934824e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">38</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0042_R_d45934824e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">42</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0043_R_d45934824e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Sci. China Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315498-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/07da3411-497a-4c3d-bc64-e442f2f2fba6/adfm202315498-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/07da3411-497a-4c3d-bc64-e442f2f2fba6/adfm202315498-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/be5c1ea9-f715-43ce-8993-3384b97fb98c/adfm202315498-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/07da3411-497a-4c3d-bc64-e442f2f2fba6/adfm202315498-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315498-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315498" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of NVP||HC full batteries. a) Rate performance of NVP||HC full batteries with different electrolytes at −40&nbsp;°C. X-ray diffraction patterns and digital photographs (insets) of HC anodes with b) 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME and c) 0.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2 electrolytes, both dissembled from corresponding NVP||HC full batteries in (a). d) Ragone plots (energy density versus power density) of the full battery with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte and previously reported SIBs at 25, −20, −40&nbsp;°C (all based on the total mass of active materials on anode and cathode). e) Cycling stability of the full battery with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte at 0.5&nbsp;C, −40&nbsp;°C (activated at 0.05&nbsp;C for the initial five cycles). f) Capacity retention rate of the NVP||HC full battery with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte and typical Na batteries at −40&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrochemical performance of HC anodes is widely recognized as the bottleneck for the fast charging and low-temperature SIBs due to the low-potential plateau of HC anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0025_R_d45934824e790" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Environ. Sci.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, rate performance of HC was evaluated based on HC||Na half-cells in the aforementioned five electrolytes. The half-cell with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte shows much higher reversible capacity from 0.06 to 2.10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at −40&nbsp;°C, greatly surpassing that of the half-cells in the other four electrolytes (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0002">2a,b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). Around 91.3% reversible capacity can be maintained at −40&nbsp;°C compared to the room-temperature capacity (310&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at 0.06&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, the HC anode with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte yields an outstanding capacity of 97&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at 2.10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and −40&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0032_R_d45934824e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0044_R_d45934824e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0045_R_d45934824e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, it can stably work up to 15, 6, and 3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0, −20, and −30&nbsp;°C, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0002">2c,d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), representing an impressive low-temperature rate performance among the carbon-based materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0002">2e</a>). To confirm the compatibility between the designed electrolyte and the HC anode, a long-term cycling test at 0.30&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and −40&nbsp;°C was performed after activation at 0.06&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0002">2f,g</a>). After 1000 cycles, HC retained a specific capacity up to 280.70&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 99.50%, superior to that of previously reported SIB anode materials at −40&nbsp;°C<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0016_R_d45934824e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0032_R_d45934824e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0034_R_d45934824e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0046_R_d45934824e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem. Eng. J.">46</a>-<a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0049_R_d45934824e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0002">2h</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315498-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/27a03e1e-9897-42a1-ad9b-27ef0e72ea57/adfm202315498-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/27a03e1e-9897-42a1-ad9b-27ef0e72ea57/adfm202315498-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/85c5804b-8739-4b4e-8a1c-1e11909aed14/adfm202315498-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/27a03e1e-9897-42a1-ad9b-27ef0e72ea57/adfm202315498-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315498-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315498" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of HC. a) Rate performance of HC anodes with five electrolytes at −40&nbsp;°C. b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge voltage profiles of HC anode with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte from 0.06&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> to 2.10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at −40&nbsp;°C. c) Rate performance of HC anode with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte at 0&nbsp;°C. d) Specific capacity of HC with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte at various current densities and temperatures. e) Rate capability of the HC anode with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte and previously reported HC anodes at −20 and −40&nbsp;°C. f) Cycling stability of HC||Na half-cell with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte at 0.30&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, −40&nbsp;°C, and g) corresponding charge/discharge profiles at different cycling numbers. h) Comparison of capacity retention rate between this work and typical SIB anodes at −40&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To gain insight into the mechanism that affects the rate performance of HC||Na half-cells at low temperatures, physicochemical properties including ionic conductivity (𝜎) and t<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-DME, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolytes have been elaborately investigated. Even at −40&nbsp;°C, the 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte still possesses a high 𝜎 of 13.87&nbsp;mS&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0003">3a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information) and t<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of 0.32 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), showing fast Na<sup>+</sup> conduction in the designed electrolyte. Furthermore, solvation entropies estimated through temperature coefficients of non-isothermal batteries<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0050_R_d45934824e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggest that 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME possesses the highest solvation entropy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0003">3c</a>), leading to a small Na<sup>+</sup> first solvation shell radius of merely 6.10&nbsp;Å (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0003">3d–f</a>). In addition, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME remains liquid at −40&nbsp;°C, which can be explained by the positive entropy change from 25 to −40&nbsp;°C via density functional theory (DFT) calculations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0003">3g</a>). The synergistic effect of positive entropy change and small solvation cluster size greatly facilitates ion transport and reduces concentration polarization at low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0051_R_d45934824e983" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nat. Energy">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structures of the three electrolytes, solvation free energy (ΔG<sub>solvation</sub>) measurements were conducted (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0003">3h</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0052_R_d45934824e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The designed electrolyte exhibits a more positive ΔG<sub>solvation</sub> value compared to the other two electrolytes, indicating that more anions participate in the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation shell. This is further supported by the molecular dynamics (MD) simulations and Raman spectra of the electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0003">3i</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information), where a coordination number (1.4) of Na<sup>+</sup> to F(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>) and contact-ion pairs (CIP) ratio (48.74%) can be found in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME, higher than the other two electrolytes. These evidences indicate the enhanced CIP solvation structure in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME, which may facilitate the formation of anion-derived SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0053_R_d45934824e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME exhibits a relatively stable electrochemical window up to 4.65&nbsp;V (versus&nbsp;Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at 25&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315498-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0d8dd26c-6516-48e4-b92c-61286985fa28/adfm202315498-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0d8dd26c-6516-48e4-b92c-61286985fa28/adfm202315498-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6384d44d-8642-4316-bdd8-787681faf9c6/adfm202315498-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0d8dd26c-6516-48e4-b92c-61286985fa28/adfm202315498-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315498-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315498" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Physicochemical properties of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-DME. a) Ionic conductivities for the three electrolytes from 25 to −40&nbsp;°C. b) Na<sup>+</sup> transference number of the three electrolytes at −40&nbsp;°C. c) Temperature coefficients of the three electrolytes, signified by slopes of the linear regressed lines. Size of Na<sup>+</sup> solvation clusters in d) 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME, e) 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2, and f) 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-DME. g) The calculated entropy change values of solvation structures in the three electrolytes from 25 to −40&nbsp;°C, the inset is the digital photograph of the three electrolytes stored at −40&nbsp;°C. h) Measured open-circuit voltage and corresponding ΔG<sub>solvation</sub> of the three electrolytes at −40&nbsp;°C. i) Na<sup>+</sup> radial distribution function obtained from MD simulations for 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In addition to the enhanced ionic transport in electrolyte, the interfacial charge transfer kinetics of HC is also crucial for the rate performance of HC anodes at low temperatures. Hence, in situ electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) measurement of HC||Na half-cell with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte at −40&nbsp;°C was conducted (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0004">4a–c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0054_R_d45934824e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The small R<sub>SEI</sub> (the resistance for Na<sup>+</sup> through SEI) values during the charge/discharge process suggest rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transportation through the SEI layer. Furthermore, the charge-transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) gradually decreases from an initial value of 128–6.84&nbsp;Ω at 0.01&nbsp;V during the discharge process, guaranteeing a low electrochemical polarization (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0004">4d</a>). In addition, the activation energies of charge transfer process at three typical states 0.40, 0.06, and 0.01&nbsp;V were calculated to be 0.056, 0.034, and 0.033&nbsp;kJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup> respectively, from the fitted Arrhenius diagram (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0004">4e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Small R<sub>ct</sub> and charge transfer activation energy can be attributed to the exceptional rate performance of HC in the designed electrolyte even at sub-freezing temperatures. To avoid the disturbance of Na metal counter electrode, symmetrical HC||HC cells have been assembled to monitor the interfacial resistance at different states of discharge (SOD) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0004">4f</a>). Similar to the aforementioned EIS measurements, the interfacial resistance gradually decreases with increasing SOD, demonstrating fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the SEI and enhanced charge transfer kinetics during the discharge process. DFT calculations were further carried out to illuminate the underlying mechanism. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers from the HC surface adsorption, intercalation between HC layers to pore filling-sodium clusters are 0.91, 0.18, and 0.10&nbsp;eV, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0004">4g,h</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17–S19</a>, Supporting Information), which are in great accordance with the change of interfacial resistance at different SOD. Overall, the reduced R<sub>ct</sub> values, diminished activation energies, and lowered Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers serve to facilitate fast Na<sup>+</sup> storage of HC at low temperatures.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315498-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7c37010e-e4ca-478e-81ff-a432b0e24e5f/adfm202315498-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7c37010e-e4ca-478e-81ff-a432b0e24e5f/adfm202315498-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bb04204e-3912-4949-a214-d5e6ae75a759/adfm202315498-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7c37010e-e4ca-478e-81ff-a432b0e24e5f/adfm202315498-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315498-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315498" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Interfacial charge transfer kinetics of HC anode at −40&nbsp;°C. a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curve and b) in situ EIS profiles of HC||Na cells measured accordingly at −40&nbsp;°C. c) A locally magnified profile for (b). d) The evolution of R<sub>S</sub>, R<sub>SEI,</sub> and R<sub>ct</sub> at different potentials derived from (b). e) Arrhenius plots and calculated activation energy obtained from the Nyquist plot of HC anode at 0.40, 0.06, and 0.01&nbsp;V. f) Nyquist plot of HC||HC cells at 0, 25, 50, 75, 100% SOD and −40&nbsp;°C. g) The calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier and h) corresponding schematic illustration of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in HC layers and Na clusters.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further reveal the origin of the low interfacial resistance, SEI composition and distribution on HC anode in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME at 25 and −40&nbsp;°C have been characterized via X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The average thickness of the SEI layer formed on the HC surface is only 5.08&nbsp;nm, which is consistent with the low R<sub>SEI</sub> value and hence accounts for the fast interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics at low temperatures (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0005">5a</a>). Scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) reveals an abundant distribution of F and O elements on the surface of HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0005">5b,c</a>). Moreover, the F 1s spectra collected from the SEI exhibit a stronger NaF signal (685.30&nbsp;eV) with etching, indicating increased inorganic content from surface to the inner SEI layer at −40&nbsp;°C, whereas the organic species display an opposite trend based on the O 1s and C 1s spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0005">5d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). The presence of NaF can be attributed to the reductive decomposition of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> from the CIP solvation structure in the designed electrolyte, which is favorable for lowering diffusion activation energy and improving the mechanical strength of SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315498-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315498-bib-0032_R_d45934824e1226" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the trend of SEI atomic ratio change with etching at 25&nbsp;°C is similar to that at −40&nbsp;°C, indicating that the SEI compositions in the designed electrolyte are highly temperature-stable (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315498-fig-0005">5e</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21 and S22</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315498-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/edb18fe0-9087-4c0c-b58c-acfa8e5fee52/adfm202315498-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/edb18fe0-9087-4c0c-b58c-acfa8e5fee52/adfm202315498-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2128de7e-3cab-40b3-b97f-8033e3073d93/adfm202315498-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/edb18fe0-9087-4c0c-b58c-acfa8e5fee52/adfm202315498-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315498-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315498" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterizations of the SEI film for the cycled HC anode with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte at −40&nbsp;°C. a) HRTEM image of the SEI film on HC anode. STEM images of b) C, O, F elements mapping and c) line scan of the SEI film. d) In-depth F 1s, O 1s, and C 1s XPS spectra and e) atomic ratio (C, O, P, F, Na) of the SEI with different Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering time.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Overall, an unprecedented high-power-density rechargeable NVP||HC full battery has been realized at −40&nbsp;°C without Na plating on HC anode surface, which can be attributed to the following three aspects: a) the designed high-solvation-entropy electrolyte (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME) with a small solvation cluster size possesses elevated ionic conductivity and larger t<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> value at low temperatures compared with conventional electrolytes, facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> migration to the HC surface; b) a thin, NaF-rich SEI layer with small interfacial resistance is generated on the HC anode surface in the designed electrolyte, promoting Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the SEI; c) fast kinetics lowers the electrochemical polarization, hence suppressing Na plating on the HC anode.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315498-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315498-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we successfully realize a high-power-density rechargeable sodium-ion full battery operated at −40&nbsp;°C without Na plating by rational electrolyte modulation. 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G2/DME electrolyte with small solvation clusters size was designed, enabling high 𝜎 and t<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> at low temperatures. Notably, the enhanced CIP structure of the designed electrolyte induces the formation of a thin, anion-derived NaF-rich SEI layer on HC anode with small interfacial resistance, facilitating facile Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the SEI. As a result, the HC anode in the designed electrolyte presents a remarkably high capacity of 284&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (0.06&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and 97&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (2.10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) at −40&nbsp;°C, respectively. Impressively, the NVP||HC full battery achieves a record-high power density of 1132.31&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (with an energy density of 61.94&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) at −40&nbsp;°C and exhibits an outstanding capacity retention of 80.60% over 1400 cycles at −40&nbsp;°C. This work would facilitate the development of low-temperature SIBs for practical power-intensive application scenarios.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
